        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer32363927" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6266532078372930066">Maybe I'm missing something, or I'm too young, or maybe nothing can live up to the expectations I had for this after Housekeeping and Gilead, or maybe I read it too fast, but I seriously found this book incredibly boring. I liked the beginning and the end, and the book does a good job of building the relationship between estranged brother and sister slowly, almost imperceptibly, over time, which felt very true. But mostly reading this book felt like watching a play in which all the characters re</span>
  <span id="freeText6266532078372930066" style="display:none">Maybe I'm missing something, or I'm too young, or maybe nothing can live up to the expectations I had for this after Housekeeping and Gilead, or maybe I read it too fast, but I seriously found this book incredibly boring. I liked the beginning and the end, and the book does a good job of building the relationship between estranged brother and sister slowly, almost imperceptibly, over time, which felt very true. But mostly reading this book felt like watching a play in which all the characters repress their stupid feelings all the time and never say what they mean and have really boring conversations for three acts and then go on being sad. And, yeah, Gilead's a boring book, too, but it's so beautiful and revelatory that it doesn't matter that basically nothing happens. But when you're writing in scene and dialogue, it's a lot harder to keep me interested in nothing happening, and Home sure didn't. Mostly it just made me feel like a big jerk for not liking it.</span>

<div id="review_752353334" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/752353334" />
    <a title="Jr Bacdayan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16587936-jr-bacdayan"><img alt="Jr Bacdayan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1552066595p2/16587936.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/752353334?book_show_action=true">Oct 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jr Bacdayan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jr Bacdayan" href="/user/show/16587936-jr-bacdayan">Jr Bacdayan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312428549" href="/book/show/6550115-home">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer752353334" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1586820409877003331">I had this unmistakable feeling of sunshine while I read this soulful novel. I always felt as though I was reading it in the morning, just at that exact moment when the majestic rays of the rising sun feel most wonderful. Not too hot, not too bright, just right. It was radiant, I felt like it gave a stir to my bones, an energy of some sort, a source of strength. It's funny really, because the book treads on a slow, probably careful albeit graceful pace. It would probably have been a five-star, h</span>
  <span id="freeText1586820409877003331" style="display:none">I had this unmistakable feeling of sunshine while I read this soulful novel. I always felt as though I was reading it in the morning, just at that exact moment when the majestic rays of the rising sun feel most wonderful. Not too hot, not too bright, just right. It was radiant, I felt like it gave a stir to my bones, an energy of some sort, a source of strength. It's funny really, because the book treads on a slow, probably careful albeit graceful pace. It would probably have been a five-star, had it not spent much time serving the appetizer before getting to the main course. Mind you, though, this appetizer is really most delectable. It's one of those stories that graph a flat line till the much awaited climax. What's interesting to note is that Madame Robinson writes with a rich and resonant tone that nullifies the evenness and stagnancy of the tale. I was conscious of the slow progress, yet I never once felt bored. <br /><br />This novel is a meditation on human capacity. It questions the limits of our ability to forgive. It delves into the cracks of our self-control. It wallows in the nature of empathy and the reasons behind it. It measures the distance that unrequited love will walk. It examines regret and why we do things that lead to it. And it presents us with the reality that self-destruction isn't limited to the destruction of one's self. That the shrapnel of destruction reaches those people that care for you, that love you, that you destroy them as well. <br /><br />Religion plays an important part in this story. The tale actually revolves around the estranged family of a reverend. So as can be expected, theology is used as medium when looking through the problems aforementioned. To my unbelieving comrades, no worries, an unbelieving son equalizes the playing field. He is often playfully sarcastic to the point of heresy. Doctrines are questioned and scriptures are misused, but he means well. It was one of the most important moments for me when the unbelieving son says he doesn't quite believe, but he understands. <br /><br />“It is possible to know the great truths without feeling the truth of them.”<br /><br />We all have differences of opinion, it should not be our aim to convert others to our view but rather to make them understand. Coming from a very religious family, I am quite different in my views with them. I still believe in God, but I am not one with certain things they believe in, especially those that appear rather dogmatic, but I understand why they believe such things and I respect it. Enough about me. Now, there are especially interesting discussions about the soul, grace, perdition, predestination, standard controversial theology. But it provides these, this spirituality in a certain light that doesn't glare at those who read it. It suffuses this spirituality with humanity that feels natural, in a way. It feels almost personal. <br /><br />Yet at the heart of it all, this is about healing and hope in the very place of the ones we hold dear, family. <br /><br />All this set in the midst of a very normal, some would say boring, life. But that's reality, life isn't filled with surprises, plot twists, and sudden reveals. Life is often repetitive, a cycle on a day-to-day basis, minor in its alterations. The greatness of Marilynne Robinson lies with the fact that she writes such breath-taking novels based on this normalcy, this circularity that many of us hate. She opens our eyes on the potential beauty of real life. Her language is evanescent yet elegant, suffusing richness and familiarity that really feels, in a way, homely. The simplicity of it all, both prose and plot, is its strength, enriching and enlivening the reader. In this way, the novel is as real as it gets. It feels so genuine, thus having the rare power of producing tears in the most commonplace events. I remember being reduced to pieces by a joke, a simple joke. This master-work touched me countless of times, especially in the latter half of it. It is rarely acknowledged, but there are a great many books that are self-conscious of their produced melancholy, books that beg you to well-up and cry a river. We are often aware of these things, yet can't still help ourselves. Oh, the drama. But, I assure you this is not of those kinds. <br /><br />This will warm your heart, fill your body with joy, your thoughts with appreciation, with a certain grace, with that fleeting feeling of faith, maybe not in God, but in life. That it goes on. That there's always hope. That redemption is always at hand no matter what.<br /><br />"There's so much to be grateful for..."</span>
  <a data-text-id="1586820409877003331" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12302811923279345052">A perfect companion to the awful <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/68210.Gilead__Gilead___1_' title='Gilead (Gilead, #1) by Marilynne Robinson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Gilead</a>. <br/><br/>Again, it is well-written, and the main character has a lot more to offer, being the lost son of the preacher's friend.   <span id="freeText12302811923279345052" style="display:none">A perfect companion to the awful <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/68210.Gilead__Gilead___1_" title="Gilead (Gilead, #1) by Marilynne Robinson" rel="nofollow noopener">Gilead</a>. <br /><br />Again, it is well-written, and the main character has a lot more to offer, being the lost son of the preacher's friend. He comes home and tries to live up to the moral and religious standards of his family, and the story could have been really good if Christian absolute truth hadn't been imposed on the main character and reader alike ALL THE TIME. I found myself yelling at the poor sinful son: "If you say SORRY just once more, I will throw this book out the window!"<br /><br />"Sorry", he answered, and the book went back to the library. <br /><br />I finished it, but apart from the relief that the main character leaves his old home again in the end, it wasn't very rewarding. The old ideas win, over and over again, they are able to destroy the energy and will to live of those who can't embrace the doctrines of righteousness and complete and utter boredom.</span>

<div id="review_1359632301" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1359632301" />
    <a title="Cathrine ☯️ " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22600410-cathrine"><img alt="Cathrine ☯️ " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582396518p2/22600410.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1359632301?book_show_action=true">Aug 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cathrine ☯️ " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cathrine ☯️ " href="/user/show/22600410-cathrine">Cathrine ☯️ </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22600410-cathrine?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1359632301" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10632903438940809566"><br/>After sleeping with the emotional state this book left me in, I have edited my review and changed it to a solid five stars. <br/><br/><i>"It is a book unsparing in its acknowledgment of sin and unstinting in its belief in the possibility of grace. It is at once hard and forgiving, bitter and joyful, fanatical and serene. “</i><br/>From "The Return Of The Prodigal Son.” A New York Times book review by A. O. Scott<br/><br/>I just don’t know what I can say or add about this book. Really. It broke my heart and I loved it anyway.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1508838035" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1508838035" />
    <a title="Ned" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21232850-ned"><img alt="Ned" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1526900273p2/21232850.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1508838035?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ned" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ned" href="/user/show/21232850-ned">Ned</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1508838035" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1293297799819727169">This hit me right in the heart. The characters are so deep and finely drawn, yet never overstated, always with the subtle threads of their actions and the revealing dialogue. I grew up in similar climes, in smallish towns in the Midwest, where it all happens. Robinson treats believers with great respect; she recapitulates their quiet and earnest struggles with truth, god and mortality in a special way. There’s not a lot of plot here, in fact some might find this absolutely dry and plodding. But </span>
  <span id="freeText1293297799819727169" style="display:none">This hit me right in the heart. The characters are so deep and finely drawn, yet never overstated, always with the subtle threads of their actions and the revealing dialogue. I grew up in similar climes, in smallish towns in the Midwest, where it all happens. Robinson treats believers with great respect; she recapitulates their quiet and earnest struggles with truth, god and mortality in a special way. There’s not a lot of plot here, in fact some might find this absolutely dry and plodding. But I know these people, they are mine, and they are true. The “good” daughter, Glory, youngest of eight, returns to take care of her aging father, the former reverend. He is still preternaturally bereft of his one lost sheep, his troubled son Jack, who suddenly appears. Glory and Jack thus find themselves in middle age, stuck in their childhood home together with their father in a final struggle to understand each other, and time is running out. The father’s health is failing and Jack is returning after 20 years of total absence, not even showing for his mother’s funeral. There’s a lifetime of fresh tension to unpack here, and the history is revealed slowly and cleverly as the estranged try to make sense of “home” and family. Jack is a seeker, but he does not find, and his tragedy permeates the lives of the others, and the extended family, plus his father’s best friend (the protagonist in the first story, Gilead). <br /><br />Robinson builds this slowly, almost in an old-fashioned way, but with deft writing and relentless honesty. It’s a beautiful and tragic story, and how it turns out is totally unpredictable (my favorite type of novel). It takes on the biggest questions of predestination and the essential goodness of God. I could relate the mental contortions of Jack, who knows the bible far better than his father and his father’s friend, and shyly takes them to task in a way that disturbs them deeply. His intelligence and wit is tragically lost on his sense of failure, keeping him on the edge of alcoholism and senseless petty crime. The first book, Gilead, introduces the fearful Jack as a sinister force – but Home shows the true character of this fated figure who can’t find redemption and can’t escape the judgment of those he loves. There is a kinship in the brother and sister, but both are nervous and awkward, despite only having each other to confide in.<br /><br />This book may make you cry, the desperate gaunt Jack who can’t find love, his sister who laments her inability to help him, and the father who cannot pull his one failure back into the fold. I underlined so many great passages. This is the second in a series and it makes me want to go back to the first to see what I missed. If the third (Lila) is like the first two, the triad will be like one beautiful epic. It’s the kind of story and writing that makes me love to read. There’s goodness and heartache and all the things we experience, and told artfully and, most importantly, honestly. There aren’t many books like this in the world. <br /><br /> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1293297799819727169" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_217447430" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/217447430" />
    <a title="Laysee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4863523-laysee"><img alt="Laysee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459741824p2/4863523.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/217447430?book_show_action=true">Sep 30, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laysee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laysee" href="/user/show/4863523-laysee">Laysee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781429929042" href="/book/show/6985516-home">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer217447430" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10253873426549523848">“Home” is set in Gilead, Iowa, of the sunflowers. It tells the story of “greying children” taking care of their “ancient father”. Glory, aged 38, the youngest of 6 children, returned home to live with her father, the Reverend Robert Boughton, after her marriage fell apart. Her brother, Jack, who had a reputation of being a scoundrel also returned home from his wandering and bitterness after 20 years. Both were unprepared to encounter a father so sad and old. Robinson wrote with empathic apprecia</span>
  <span id="freeText10253873426549523848" style="display:none">“Home” is set in Gilead, Iowa, of the sunflowers. It tells the story of “greying children” taking care of their “ancient father”. Glory, aged 38, the youngest of 6 children, returned home to live with her father, the Reverend Robert Boughton, after her marriage fell apart. Her brother, Jack, who had a reputation of being a scoundrel also returned home from his wandering and bitterness after 20 years. Both were unprepared to encounter a father so sad and old. Robinson wrote with empathic appreciation of the struggles familiar to individuals coming to terms with the infirmities of aging, the pain of care giving, and the heartache of loving people closest to oneself. One of the questions that emerged for me as I read this novel was: Why does caring for someone hurt so much? Robinson astutely captured the pathos and irony of home – “What kinder place could there be on earth, and why did it seem to them all like exile?” Indeed, “Home” poses a few hard questions. For Jack, the question is: How does a person live out the rest of one's life when one has done something terrible and it cannot be undone? There are no answers to such questions.<br /><br />“Home” is a novel one should read after “Gilead”. In Gilead, Jack Boughton was perceived from the perspective of the Reverend Ames as an incorrigible trouble maker whom he feared would corrupt his wife and young son. In Home, Robinson afforded a fresh set of lenses that allowed us to see Jack in a different light. It’s all about perspective. Despite the roguishness we’ve come to expect of Jack, we now see him unlacing his father's shoes and carrying him to bed. We also see him struggling to come home to himself, a bone-weary man conscious of negative public opinion, trying desperately to redeem himself in his father's eyes. It is also extremely disquieting that a child like Jack brought up in a seemingly God-fearing family could be made to feel like a misfit even though he was his father’s most beloved child. By the time the novel ends, you cannot help but feel sorry for Jack and even grow to like him and wish he would have another chance to turn his life around.<br /><br />I guess “Home” is likely to affect the reader deeply in different ways depending on his or her experiences and where s/he is along the continuum of living and dying. That said, there is the unmistakable, underlying thread of communion of being human, finding forgiveness, and learning to be thankful for the little blessings of an imperfect life. Robinson said it well, "There is so much to be grateful for, words are poor things." <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10253873426549523848" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_398907296" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/398907296" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4086118-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567611029p2/4086118.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/398907296?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/4086118-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=family-relationships">family-relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=spirituality">spirituality</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=iowa">iowa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=rural">rural</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer398907296" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer278066064307748249">I was disappointed with the muted passions of the characters in the novel and their unrealistic dwelling on spiritual and moral issues in isolation from the wellsprings of the usual ongoing connections and concerns in life. The prose has the same elegant and spare virtues as Robinson’s previous books, “Housekeeping” and “Gilead”, but the narrative often dragged to me due to excessive length relative to few substantive events and choices in action taken by the characters. <br/><br/>In the late 50’s, a duti</span>
  <span id="freeText278066064307748249" style="display:none">I was disappointed with the muted passions of the characters in the novel and their unrealistic dwelling on spiritual and moral issues in isolation from the wellsprings of the usual ongoing connections and concerns in life. The prose has the same elegant and spare virtues as Robinson’s previous books, “Housekeeping” and “Gilead”, but the narrative often dragged to me due to excessive length relative to few substantive events and choices in action taken by the characters. <br /><br />In the late 50’s, a dutiful middle-aged daughter, Glory, leaves her teaching job to returns to rural Gilead, Iowa, to take care of her frail elderly father, a saint-like pillar of the community he served as a Presbytarian minister. They are joined by an extended visit of the prodigal oldest son, Jack, who was a troublemaker in his youth and has been a source of worry while out of touch for nearly two decades. Outwardly, Christian kindness, forgiveness, and hope for redemption abounds in all their interactions, yet each of the three struggle with their largely hidden senses of guilt over past failures and inadequacies in their current roles in life. <br /><br />As the characters move toward a better understanding of each other, the reader is faced with a claustrophobia inducing sense of isolation. Aside from regular interactions of daily home life, there is little impingement of the outside world for most of the novel. When Jack and Glory refer to their past lives, no names or places are revealed. A TV is acquired, providing and small window of discussion about the ongoing conflicts of the Civil Rights movement. A rhythm of home life is marked by Jack regularly working on an old car and he and Glory tending daily to the garden. No one fights or displays strong emotions when they are together. No serious medical illnesses of the father calls for dramatic efforts in his care. <br /><br />There is beauty in this innovative model for exploring the meaning of “home” and exploration of whether adults can truly return to it. But the story almost leans toward the absurdist minimalism of “Waiting for Godot” or to the philosophical setting of Plato’s Cave. But for me I need more realism or more content in the story to reap full pleasures of the resonance between the universal and particulars of life.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="278066064307748249" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1877756127" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1877756127" />
    <a title="Gumble&#39;s Yard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35482263-gumble-s-yard"><img alt="Gumble&#39;s Yard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604226927p2/35482263.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1877756127?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gumble&#39;s Yard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gumble&#39;s Yard" href="/user/show/35482263-gumble-s-yard">Gumble&#39;s Yard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35482263-gumble-s-yard?shelf=2015">2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35482263-gumble-s-yard?shelf=women-prize-winners">women-prize-winners</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1877756127" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3155343317993161665">A companion novel to “Gilead” told in the third person and telling the story of Jack Broughton, his father Rev Broughton and particularly his youngest sister – Glory, living back at home after her fiancée of many years finally stopped his deceit of her (admitting he was married). Many of the scenes directly overlap with “Home” but are viewed by other observers – particularly the interactions between Jack and Ames (mainly recounted by Jack to Glory).<br/><br/>As brilliantly written as “Gilead” although wit</span>
  <span id="freeText3155343317993161665" style="display:none">A companion novel to “Gilead” told in the third person and telling the story of Jack Broughton, his father Rev Broughton and particularly his youngest sister – Glory, living back at home after her fiancée of many years finally stopped his deceit of her (admitting he was married). Many of the scenes directly overlap with “Home” but are viewed by other observers – particularly the interactions between Jack and Ames (mainly recounted by Jack to Glory).<br /><br />As brilliantly written as “Gilead” although with a very different note – this is a portrayal of disappointment, of hope prolonged and ultimately disappointed, of the difficult of forgiveness and of being forgiven and of forgiving oneself, of the difficult of being a non-repentant but regretful prodigal, of grace and its limitations at least in human form, of both disappointed and unformed faith, of loneliness and isolation especially in a crowd and a family, of ageing (both old age and lapsing into a middle age that didn’t match ones dreams or hopes), of redemption and pre-destination<br /><br />Ultimately a hugely moving and deeply sad novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3155343317993161665" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1670724910" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1670724910" />
    <a title="J.C." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/52123967-j-c"><img alt="J.C." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593545762p2/52123967.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1670724910?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.C." class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.C." href="/user/show/52123967-j-c">J.C.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52123967-j-c?shelf=american">american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1670724910" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2573187382402189278">I've just re-read “Home” after having been given Marilynne Robinson's latest book, "Jack", which is concerned with the years before Jack, the beloved prodigal son, returns home. "Home" is the second book in the Gilead set. He’s not the only one to have returned; his sister, Glory, has come back to look after their father, a Presbyterian minister who is now old and frail. She has her own troubled past, which she has kept hidden. Much of the book is a slow, intimate, building of the relationship b</span>
  <span id="freeText2573187382402189278" style="display:none">I've just re-read “Home” after having been given Marilynne Robinson's latest book, "Jack", which is concerned with the years before Jack, the beloved prodigal son, returns home. "Home" is the second book in the Gilead set. He’s not the only one to have returned; his sister, Glory, has come back to look after their father, a Presbyterian minister who is now old and frail. She has her own troubled past, which she has kept hidden. Much of the book is a slow, intimate, building of the relationship between Jack and the sister who was a young teenager when he left home, but who helped deal with a tragedy he left behind. There’s no swift unburdening of souls; the intensity of emotion and the attempts at becoming reconciled to one’s past are drawn from the details of life in the old house, the sense of being enchanted, or trapped, by the expectations of the past, the life one has lived, and the gentle, insistent voice of faith in the old minister and in his friend, John Ames, the Congregationalist minister whom we met in “Gilead”. No easy solutions are presented by this offered faith, and the deepest questions remain unanswered in any rational sense, but grace is always present. It pervades anything that this author creates. I loved “Gilead”, but every book I have read by this author seems to take strides further into these people she has created, and “Home” has lead me straight on to re-read the next in the series, “Lila”, which I’ll review in a few days (if I haven’t already done so!). But as I read each of them they seem more powerful than the last!<br />When I finished re-reading “Home” my first impulse was whether or not to proceed with an idea I had had, to give a copy of “Gilead” to a friend of mine who is deeply religious and who belongs to a strict church. “Gilead” is the most straightforward of the books and the central character is John Ames, a minister. I should have pointed out in my really brief review of “Gilead” that there is a fantastic figure in “Gilead” of John Ames’ grandfather, also a minister, but one who took up arms in the Civil War). I feel sure my friend would appreciate this first book in the set; but I had the strange notion that if she went on to read the other books I didn’t want Jack judged by the standards of religion as it is often observed in our society. In that way I am of course imposing my own judgement. Jack, alcoholic, thief, and ‘bum’, is a precious soul, a refinement of suffering and integrity far from the sensibilities that most of us can attain as we go about our daily lives. <br />“Home” is so very sad, and so sensitively written – Marilynne Robinson has a wonderful gift for tense dialogue, and the minutest interaction between people. She said (in a recent “Start the Week” programme on BBC Radio 4) that religion in America just now does not encourage a positive, hopeful and respectful attitude to others. These books do.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2573187382402189278" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_70951738" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/70951738" />
    <a title="Sandi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/811687-sandi"><img alt="Sandi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1404709147p2/811687.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/70951738?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sandi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sandi" href="/user/show/811687-sandi">Sandi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/811687-sandi?shelf=library-books-read">library-books-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/811687-sandi?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/811687-sandi?shelf=2009">2009</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/811687-sandi?shelf=religion">religion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer70951738" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12237937873973746981">Since joining GoodReads, my reading has become much more prolific and eclectic. Thanks to the reviews of the members here, I have been much more successful at finding books that appeal to me and are worth my time. One of the books that I read this year as a result of GoodReads reviews was “Gilead” by Marilynne Robinson. “Gilead” was one of the most profoundly moving books I have ever read. It touched my heart in a way that few books ever do. It spoke of faith and forgiveness in such a reverent m</span>
  <span id="freeText12237937873973746981" style="display:none">Since joining GoodReads, my reading has become much more prolific and eclectic. Thanks to the reviews of the members here, I have been much more successful at finding books that appeal to me and are worth my time. One of the books that I read this year as a result of GoodReads reviews was “Gilead” by Marilynne Robinson. “Gilead” was one of the most profoundly moving books I have ever read. It touched my heart in a way that few books ever do. It spoke of faith and forgiveness in such a reverent manner. I may have to review my top-10 list and find a way to make room for “Gilead” on it.<br /><br />The thing about “Gilead” is that it was a stand-alone novel. It had an ending that was natural and made sense. It left no room for a sequel. Robinson’s latest novel set in the Iowa town of Gilead, “Home”, is not a sequel. Instead, it is a parallel novel. It takes place in the same time period as “Gilead”, but is told from the point of view of the youngest Boughton child, Glory. Glory is a 38-year-old spinster who comes home to take care of her aging father. Some time after she arrives, her wayward brother, Jack, comes home after a twenty-year absence. “Home” examines the relationship that develops between Jack and Glory as they get to know each other and take care of their now-fragile father. Through Glory’s loving and accepting eyes, we get to see a side of Jack that is quite different from the person John Ames sees in “Gilead”. <br /><br />I had a few problems with “Home”. Most importantly, Robinson chose to use an third person narrator for this story instead of letting Glory tell it in first person as John Ames did in “Gilead”. While the point of view is mostly Glory’s, Robinson lets herself comment on other character’s thoughts and feelings. It seems sloppy, especially when it happens mid-paragraph. It was also strange reading about Jack when I already knew a lot of his story from “Gilead”. I knew who Della was and why he hadn’t brought her with him. Knowing what I knew, it made his reactions to certain news stories make sense. However, he never once comes out and tells Glory the information he told John Ames in “Gilead” about Della. Glory doesn’t find out until the very end though a very implausible circumstance. It was very, very odd already knowing the story of Jack as “Home” is unfolding. That said, I really don’t think “Home” stands on its own the way “Gilead” did. I think if the reader hasn’t read the first novel, there is no way he or she is going to understand what is going on in this one. On the other hand, “Gilead” totally spoils “Home”. The whole thing is given away before the reader even picks it up. I also felt that I wanted to know more about Glory and the period between childhood and her return home. We get a bit of her story, but I would have liked it to have been more substantial.<br /><br />I am giving “Home” three stars because it is beautifully written. The imagery is subtle but amazingly well done. I could see the Boughton house, garden and barn so clearly in my mind. The setting really came to life and almost became a character of its own. I liked that we got to see a bit of Lila Ames as a real person instead of an idealized wife and mother. <br /><br />I’m going to end this review with a question that kept coming to my mind. Why did the Boughtons choose Glory as the name for their youngest daughter? The other girls were named Faith, Hope and Grace. In my mind, I kept thinking that the fourth girl should be named Charity, not Glory. I thought Glory was an unusual choice.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12237937873973746981" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1609635251" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1609635251" />
    <a title="Sohaib" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31777090-sohaib"><img alt="Sohaib" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599511226p2/31777090.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1609635251?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sohaib" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sohaib" href="/user/show/31777090-sohaib">Sohaib</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781844085507" href="/book/show/6097133-home">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31777090-sohaib?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31777090-sohaib?shelf=2016-reads">2016-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1609635251" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14373609354545303455">A wonderful read! <i>Home</i> is about family, love, life and second chances. I really enjoyed Glory’s account of what happens throughout the novel, although at one point I was tempted to question the reality of what she thinks or wonder about how other characters perceive the same incidents. Her narrative, however, is remarkable, cautious and meticulous when it comes to her brother and father, adding to the familial intensity (this is called a third person limited narrator if you are wondering and I g</span>
  <span id="freeText14373609354545303455" style="display:none">A wonderful read! <i>Home</i> is about family, love, life and second chances. I really enjoyed Glory’s account of what happens throughout the novel, although at one point I was tempted to question the reality of what she thinks or wonder about how other characters perceive the same incidents. Her narrative, however, is remarkable, cautious and meticulous when it comes to her brother and father, adding to the familial intensity (this is called a third person limited narrator if you are wondering and I guess Robinson intentionally wrote it this way to fit this book dialogically into the trilogy). I only read <i>Housekeeping</i> for Robinson by the way.<br><br>The overarching theme in <i>Home</i> is God’s Providence and how our conscious perception is always hindered by what we do not know or cannot understand. Our judgments and perception of others are often skewed because of our unconscious attachment to our subjective experiences and what we do not know. After all, we can only have our own experience to give, measure and judge other people and experiences upon. It would be interesting to read <i>Lila</i> and <i>Gilead</i>; to complete the trilogy and the characters view on things – to completely understand the dialogicity of the three novels.<br><br>Putting <i>Housekeeping</i> and <i>Home</i> side by side, the two are postmodern narratives, optimistic and vivacious. <i>Housekeeping</i> focuses on the initiation phase and getting the journey going, the journey of transience for Ruth and Sylvie. <i>Home</i> focuses on the return phase, the phase where all the doing is done, where the journey is about to end and the characters have been through all the battles and have the scars to show for it now (think why Jack’s scar under his eye keeps popping up in the novel). It is interesting how Robinson shifts the traditional focus of most narratives, given that we view every novel in the journey motif frame. <i>Home</i> foregrounds what happens (or what does not happen) in the most inactive phase, the return phase. Jack’s transformation has already happened after he returns back home, but all the tension ensues because of the characters’ failure to understand this (even Jack himself) …<br><br>All in all, <i>Home</i> is a wonderful read! Can’t wait to get my hands on <i>Gilead</i> and <i>Lila</i>, especially <i>Lila</i> because of the narrator’s transient background, which is reminiscent of a some kind of sequel for <i>Housekeeping</i>’s Ruth and Sylvie.<br><br><a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by/4.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img alt="Creative Commons License" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1406704146i/10586746.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>This review is licensed under a <a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by/4.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">Creative Commons Attribution 4.0 International License</a>.</span>

<div id="review_2656725877" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2656725877" />
    <a title="Raul Bimenyimana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43664238-raul-bimenyimana"><img alt="Raul Bimenyimana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1466510542p2/43664238.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2656725877?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Raul Bimenyimana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Raul Bimenyimana" href="/user/show/43664238-raul-bimenyimana">Raul Bimenyimana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43664238-raul-bimenyimana?shelf=women-writers">women-writers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43664238-raul-bimenyimana?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2656725877" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17560704520253726321">Home, that familiar physical space and whatever it may mean and represent to different people. Comfort, love, salvation, pain, regret, disappointment and most likely more than either one of these things and yet there's no denying in some way that that space we live in and at times share in with others, often does leave a mark on us. <br/><br/>In this story siblings Glory and Jack, middle-aged and mourning their different failures have returned home to their aging father in a small town in Iowa. This is a </span>
  <span id="freeText17560704520253726321" style="display:none">Home, that familiar physical space and whatever it may mean and represent to different people. Comfort, love, salvation, pain, regret, disappointment and most likely more than either one of these things and yet there's no denying in some way that that space we live in and at times share in with others, often does leave a mark on us. <br /><br />In this story siblings Glory and Jack, middle-aged and mourning their different failures have returned home to their aging father in a small town in Iowa. This is a slow read, nothing abrupt or major in tragic proportions ever happens. Yet how to describe some passages, especially the last pages? Touching cannot be enough.<br /><br />Reading this made me think about the sentimentality vs sensitivity debates I've come across. Of course in a lot of cases the debates were all a pitiable often sexist façade to dismiss certain works but this book made me think a lot about sensitivity and sentimentality in fiction. The sentimental for the most part is overwrought, the sensitive sufficient. Toni Morrison once spoke about trusting the reader, that there are certain things although unspoken, can be entrusted to the reader to be understood. I think it requires a lot of restraint for the writer, that although they can go on describing and writing they choose not to. <br /><br />It is difficult to imagine Marilynne Robinson was not thinking about this book when she was writing Gilead. I have heard an interview where she confirms she wasn't but given how seamlessly it accompanies the first book, it is hard to see it that way. The plot runs concurrently with Gilead so that it reads more like a flipped coin than a sequel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17560704520253726321" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_32199190" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/32199190" />
    <a title="J. Trott" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/157583-j-trott"><img alt="J. Trott" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442637079p2/157583.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/32199190?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J. Trott" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J. Trott" href="/user/show/157583-j-trott">J. Trott</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer32199190" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1110266797547672590">I was not disappointed by Marilynne Robinson's third book. When one reads good literature, for instance, Cormac McCarthy, one is often struck by a turn of phrase or a passage. "How aptly and poetically written," one thinks. This happens rarely with Robinson, because her prose lead us perfectly into the characters' lives. So it is only in hindsight that one identifies her prose as beautiful, and this only because one thinks of the story itself as true in the sense that truth causes one to feel li</span>
  <span id="freeText1110266797547672590" style="display:none">I was not disappointed by Marilynne Robinson's third book. When one reads good literature, for instance, Cormac McCarthy, one is often struck by a turn of phrase or a passage. "How aptly and poetically written," one thinks. This happens rarely with Robinson, because her prose lead us perfectly into the characters' lives. So it is only in hindsight that one identifies her prose as beautiful, and this only because one thinks of the story itself as true in the sense that truth causes one to feel lifted out of oneself. <br /><br />In "Home", Robinson combines the best of "Housekeeping" and "Gilead". On one hand, she describes with her characteristically oblique poignancy the life of the outcast. On the other she describes the long good life of faith with its struggles and its blessings. However, this book is not the same as "Giliead", in which faith was is painted much more darkly here than in "Gilead". Jack, the black sheep of Pastor Boughton's eight children, returns to visit his father in his old age. His father has a hard time seeing Jack again, whom he loves deeply, but who is still an occasional drunk, among other things. Jack's little sister is also home, tending to her father in her dottage. Jack is wary of them both, expecting their accusations. <br /><br />The father, Boughton, recieves his son with joy. However, as the book wears on, there is question about whether or not he has the strength to love his prodigal son. He summons Jack to his room to reprimand him for sins imagined and remembered, despite the great pain these talks give Jack. The character of Jack is memorable, he stands with a slouching elegance and has an irony and humor in all he says, like one long accustomed to failure and judgment. Boughton reminds me of what I know of a religious parent's strong drive to see their child go on the narrow path. The well meaning, and capacity for forgiveness do not lessen the force of displeasure, but rather give it weight. The judgement of a fool, or a bad man, or an enemy is unimportant. But the displeasure of a good, loving and even forgiving father is an almost unshakeable weight. <br /><br />Glory, the little sister, is however, taken with her brother. She remembers his humor, his wistful disposition, and the rarity of his presence from when she was growing up. He however suspects her of being ready with condemnation for his life, and it is only through her sustained effort at kindness that they become friends. She is ultimately the one that recieves a few confidences from Jack, and is able to give him some small comfort. One cannot call this story happy. If there is a common thematic thread in Marilynne Robinson's novels, it is the deep pain that people carry beneath their surfaces, and need for gentleness, forgiveness, and sustained compassion. This does not end the suffering that the world gives people, but it makes it, perhaps, bearable. This is the stuff to smoke. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1110266797547672590" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_31670165" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/31670165" />
    <a title="Ron Charles" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1384944-ron-charles"><img alt="Ron Charles" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567601289p2/1384944.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/31670165?book_show_action=true">Aug 31, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ron Charles" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ron Charles" href="/user/show/1384944-ron-charles">Ron Charles</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of &quot;Gilead&quot;</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer31670165" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17813439204072625511">Marilynne Robinson's mournful new novel, Home, is not a sequel or a prequel to her Pulitzer Prize-winning Gilead (2004) but rather a companion. And companionship, it turns out, is what all the lonely people in this book are seeking. Set in the same Iowa town, just a short distance from Rev. John Ames, the dying narrator of Gilead, the events in Home take place concurrently with those of that other novel. This time, however, we're in the house of Rev. Robert Boughton, Ames's longtime friend, who'</span>
  <span id="freeText17813439204072625511" style="display:none">Marilynne Robinson's mournful new novel, Home, is not a sequel or a prequel to her Pulitzer Prize-winning Gilead (2004) but rather a companion. And companionship, it turns out, is what all the lonely people in this book are seeking. Set in the same Iowa town, just a short distance from Rev. John Ames, the dying narrator of Gilead, the events in Home take place concurrently with those of that other novel. This time, however, we're in the house of Rev. Robert Boughton, Ames's longtime friend, who's equally close to putting on "imperishability."<br /><br />The publisher claims these two novels can be read separately, but that's not fair to the profound relationship between them nor, I think, to the way Home depends on its predecessor for detail and resonance. Indeed, as meditative and spare as Gilead is, it now seems downright hyperactive next to this ruminative new volume. Rev. Ames, you will recall, spiced his reflections on life and God with wild tales of his one-eyed grandfather, who rode with the abolition terrorist John Brown. There are deadly adventures in Home, too, but they take place offstage, and they're never mentioned, only outlined by the pained silence of those who cannot forget.<br /><br />Almost all the physical movement of this story is exhausted in its opening pages with the return of Glory, the youngest of Boughton's eight children. Robinson writes in the third person, but we see the events that unfold over the following months in 1950 from Glory's point of view. Her father is overjoyed to see her, and neither of them mentions the collapsed engagement and abandoned career that have brought her back to live in her childhood house at the age of 38. "Nothing about that house ever did change," she thinks, "except to fade or scar or wear." Now, forced to abandon dreams of a husband and a child of her own, she's haunted by the question, "What does it mean to come home?" With so much nursing and housekeeping to be done, both of them can pretend that Glory has returned entirely for her father's sake. "She did not permit herself to brood, strong as the urge was sometimes," Robinson writes. "She could decide nothing about her life. She did not want to think about her life."<br /><br />Their quiet routine is soon interrupted by the return of another wayward Boughton child. The black sheep in this otherwise happy family, Jack was a petty thief and a brooding drunk who skipped town 20 years before, leaving behind his teenage girlfriend, a baby and a cloud of shame. During the intervening years, Jack continued to torture his parents by spurning every offer of assistance no matter how desperate his circumstances. When he finally returns -- thin, pale, unkempt -- Glory barely recognizes him. Though she once idolized him, now he seems to her "the weight on the family's heart, the unnamed absence, like the hero in a melancholy tale." But their father -- a man of "tireless tenderness" -- is giddy, thrilled by the possibility that his boundless love may finally open the heart of his wary, rueful child.<br /><br />This is a version of the Prodigal Son that picks up where the Gospel parable stops, after the extravagant feast, when the excitement of reunion fades in the awkwardness of the next day, and then the next. Robinson has constructed a plot so still that it seems at times more a series of tableaux than a novel. The tension in Home is palpable but invisible. Rev. Boughton, Glory and Jack move through domestic chores and hesitant conversations, fraught with the danger of confession or rapprochement or affection. Glory and her father are determined to make their love known to Jack, but the possibility of his bolting again renders them all timid and formal. "They had always been so careful of him," Robinson writes, "almost afraid to touch him. There was an aloofness about him more thoroughgoing than modesty or reticence. It was feral, and fragile."<br /><br />Jack is a man in the throes of a spiritual crisis, which Robinson captures with the most exquisite precision. An alcoholic clutching at the edges of sobriety, he's tempted to think he can clean himself up, but he's desperately afraid of failing, knowing that one more slip could kill both him and his long suffering father. With a mixture of affection, embarrassment and annoyance, he realizes that his father is "afraid to die because of me. To leave me behind, still unregenerate."<br /><br />Writing one novel about a minister's family is asking for trouble; writing a second seems downright unrepentant, the kind of misjudgment that could land a reputable literary author in a Christian bookstore or with a cozy series on the BBC. But Robinson, who teaches at the Iowa Writers' Workshop, is unlikely to suffer either fate; her books are toxic to sentimentality. Even more than their stylistic beauty, what's miraculous about Gilead and Home is their explicit focus on spiritual affliction, discussed in the hard terms of Protestant theology. Robinson uses the words "grace," "salvation" and "prayer" frequently and without embarrassment and without drifting into the gassy lingo of ecumenical spirituality. Her characters cower in the shadow of perdition.<br /><br />Though as a teenager Jack seemed to have paid no attention to his father's sermons, now, amid the ruins of his adult life, he's hypnotized by a sense of his worthlessness even as he feels "a certain spiritual hunger." Why, he wonders, could he never be a part of this wonderful family? What has drawn him again and again to hurt them and himself? "I don't really know what to do with myself," he tells Glory. "I'm a scoundrel."<br /><br />As a disquisition on the agonies of family love and serial disappointment, Home is sometimes too illuminating to bear. During a long, candid conversation that serves as the crisis of the novel, Jack's father confesses, "So many times, over the years, I've tried not to love you so much. I never got anywhere with it, but I tried." And then he manages to ask, without rancor of any kind, "What I'd like to know, is why you didn't love us. That is what has always mystified me."<br /><br />Although there's much sadness here, it's always cradled in Robinson's voice. "This life on earth is a strange business," she writes, but somehow that business sounds like a more familiar home in these discerning pages.<br /><br />Originally published in The Washington Post:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.washingtonpost.com/wp-dyn/content/article/2008/09/04/AR2008090402449.html">http://www.washingtonpost.com/wp-dyn/...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="17813439204072625511" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_31670165'><a id="like_count_review_31670165" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/31670165?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">16 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F2924318-home">Like</a></span>

<div id="review_97811046" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/97811046" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/97811046?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312428549" href="/book/show/6550115-home">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Tearjerkers fans</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die (2010 edition)
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=1001-core">1001-core</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer97811046" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8841442824994710489">Whoa, now this is the tearjerker! Last week, I read Roxana Robinson's <b>Cost</b> and mentioned in my review that although it was a heavy melodrama, it would not make you cry. Since that and this book, Marilynne Robinson's <b>Home</b> are both included in the 1001 list and the two lady authors share the same surname (Robinson - although no relation), I read them with only a book in between.<br/><br/>This is about relationships all anchored in a place we call HOME. Yes, the novel is aptly titled (unlike in <i>Cost</i> which up</span>
  <span id="freeText8841442824994710489" style="display:none">Whoa, now this is the tearjerker! Last week, I read Roxana Robinson's <b>Cost</b> and mentioned in my review that although it was a heavy melodrama, it would not make you cry. Since that and this book, Marilynne Robinson's <b>Home</b> are both included in the 1001 list and the two lady authors share the same surname (Robinson - although no relation), I read them with only a book in between.<br /><br />This is about relationships all anchored in a place we call HOME. Yes, the novel is aptly titled (unlike in <i>Cost</i> which up to now, I am not sure why it was titled that way). It's about enduring friendships between two men in cloth, i.e., Presbyterian priests. Mrs. Robinson has only written 3 novels in the span of 30 years and all those novels are award-winning. The second novel, <i>Gilead</i>, is about a preacher, Reverend Ames, who has a secondary role in this 3rd novel, <i>Home</i>. However, his friendship with this novel's main character, Reverend Robert Boughton is something that seems to be rare nowadays. It is a friendship that is true, loyal and is based on their deep faith in God. <br /><br />It is also about the relationship between siblings. Reverend Boughton and his wife (already deceased), had 8 all grownup children. In the story, all of them were mentioned and described but only 3 were active characters. These were Jack, the 45-y/o "black sheep" of the family, Glory, the 38-y/o youngest child who was ditched by her married lover and Teddy, the 43-y/o doctor who was the brightest in the family. The storytelling of Mrs. Robinson is so pure and lucid that you will hang on to each of her word and keep on turning each page as you are mesmerized with her beautiful prose. For me, what made her different from all the contemporary authors I've so far read is the way she builds her climax. If you are the reader who wants action in every page, this is not the book for you. There are no big dramatic dialogues, no earth-shaking events, no historical backdrop but you keep on turning the page because you always have that feeling that at any point, something big will come up. It's like there is a dagger that is lurking above the characters despite all of them being gentle, polite and kind to each other. Almost everything happened in their old family house located in a fictional town of Gilead in Iowa sometime in the 50's when life was a lot simpler and houses in heartland USA were still all surrounded by trees, garden and with a porch and a barn. The type that you see in the movies set in that era.<br /><br />Above all, this is about HOME. <i>Ye, who are weary, come home</i> says Glory to Jack at their farewell scene. That part that made me cry this morning. It was my fault. When reading a sentimental novel and I know I was nearing the climax, I play a mood music. You know what? All the characters in this book are cry babies. So, why not join the fun, I told myself. Just like in the movies the soundtrack can enhance the scene. This morning, I played the album "Classical Heartbreakers" and when I was on the last 20 pages, I was already one of the characters - tears swelling in my eyes. <br /><br />Read this. Reminisce the time that you were young and living with your parents and siblings. If you are not in this kind of family drama, just keep on reading and in the last 20 pages, I know you will cry. You will.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8841442824994710489" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_114595445" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/114595445" />
    <a title="Barbara H" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1883816-barbara-h"><img alt="Barbara H" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572546566p2/1883816.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/114595445?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Barbara H" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Barbara H" href="/user/show/1883816-barbara-h">Barbara H</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer114595445" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4470072061657816283">Here I was about half way into this book. I received numerous recommendations from my GR Friends, which usually leads me to pleasurable reading. Most of the beginning felt as if I were slogging through a deep morass. I found it tedious. I continued because I expected something would come to life, perhaps on the next page? Or the next? <i> Finally, </i> on page 151 there were definite glimmers! I'll save further criticisms for my review.<br/><br/>Stangely, considering the title subject, I kept reverting to though</span>
  <span id="freeText4470072061657816283" style="display:none">Here I was about half way into this book. I received numerous recommendations from my GR Friends, which usually leads me to pleasurable reading. Most of the beginning felt as if I were slogging through a deep morass. I found it tedious. I continued because I expected something would come to life, perhaps on the next page? Or the next? <i> Finally, </i> on page 151 there were definite glimmers! I'll save further criticisms for my review.<br /><br />Stangely, considering the title subject, I kept reverting to thoughts of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/60471.Coming_Home" title="Coming Home by Rosamunde Pilcher" rel="nofollow noopener">Coming Home</a>, which I tried to read several times, but could never complete it. Although the stories differ, I had the same sense of Pilcher's work. It just did not seem to be going anywhere!<br /><br />*************************<br /><br />Mailynne Robinson has obviously exerted much effort in writing this book. Her character studies are thorough, probing and provide clear analysis. Her knowledge of religion and the Bible is obviously seasoned and meditative. She capably imparted the atmosphere and the environment where those in her story lived. <br /><br />After acknowledging many of the positive qualities of this novel, I must state that this was not an entirely pleasant reading experience for me. I am not going to give a plot summary here, merely my points of view. It seemed to me that Robinson had taken far too long to develop her plot. Much of what was said, although her words were well stated, could have been pared down. This factor made it difficult to maintain interest and attention to what she was trying to relate.<br /><br />Another problem for me was that although I enjoy reading and learning about cultures and religions other than my own, the book continued page after page with Biblical tales, sermonizing and homilizing. Granted, the story involved a minister's family, but their lives seemed to have no other interests. As Glory, the main female character has started to think, "...speaking the dialect of their slightly self-enamored and distinctly clerical family..."<br /><br />Robinson has clearly spelled out what it means to come home, but this home seems to contain much discontent. Glory's role seems to be that of the Peacemaker and Housekeeper, as she attempts to ensure happiness for everyone else.We barely discovered her own interests and desires. Jack, the "prodigal son", has returned home after a mystifying absence of 20 years, with sketchy hints about the intervening time. We discover that he was always considered the bad boy of the eight children in this family, but it eventually becomes evident that he always lived in a state of unhappiness and unease with the routines and the ideals in the home. Robinson illustrates throughout his awkwardness and inability to express or earn his father's or siblings' love.<br /><br /><i> Home </i> is a sad and thought provoking novel. Although I grant that this author has written well and should earn at least 3 stars, I am giving this 2 stars for the content, which took too long to achieve my interest.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4470072061657816283" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Ye5IgSiXiYz6HwC1oF1ONmwwvXF5VfIF1FyxoduUE+ZdRN1iKz+LjIxTTvL2vC86sa0gnfA5XXnPD7PL+rOQHg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('wZJ3r3tV3VrDJCPsMBk9AJ+jKe8AqB1l+60sPh+QKmr9OOJMeP3fWrVobatm+FwMQj60A4nEshng/i5UPrepkg==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Auden once wrote that “art is our chief means of breaking bread with th<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText15186425572103306528\" style=\"display:none\">From the author of HOW TO THINK and THE PLEASURES OF READING IN AN AGE OF DISTRACTION, a literary guide to engaging with the voices of the past to stay sane in the present\n\nW. H. Auden once wrote that “art is our chief means of breaking bread with the dead.” In his brilliant and compulsively readable new treatise, Breaking Bread with the Dead, Alan Jacobs shows us that engaging with the strange and wonderful writings of the past might help us live less anxiously in the present—and increase what Thomas Pynchon once called our “personal density.”\n\nToday we are battling too much information in a society changing at lightning speed, with algorithms aimed at shaping our every thought—plus a sense that history offers no resources, only impediments to overcome or ignore. The modern solution to our problems is to surround ourselves only with what we know and what brings us instant comfort. Jacobs’s answer is the opposite: to be in conversation with, and challenged by, those from the past who can tell us what we never thought we needed to know.\n\nWhat can Homer teach us about force? How does Frederick Douglass deal with the massive blind spots of America’s Founding Fathers? And what can we learn from modern authors who engage passionately and profoundly with the past? How can Ursula K. Le Guin show us truths about Virgil’s female characters that Virgil himself could never have seen? In Breaking Bread with the Dead, a gifted scholar draws us into close and sympathetic engagement with texts from across the ages, including the work of Anita Desai, Henrik Ibsen, Jean Rhys, Simone Weil, Edith Wharton, Amitav Ghosh, Claude Lévi-Strauss, Italo Calvino, and many more.\n\nBy hearing the voices of the past, we can expand our consciousness, our sympathies, and our wisdom far beyond what our present moment can offer.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"15186425572103306528\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17399047735137095918">Marilynne Summers Robinson (born November 26, 1943) is an American novelist and essayist. Across her writing career, Robinson has received numerous awards, including the Pulitzer Prize for Fiction in 2005, National Humanities Medal in 2012, and the 2016 Library of Congress Prize for American Fiction. In 2016, Robinson was named in Time magazine's list of 100 most influential people.[2] Robinson be</span>
  <span id="freeText17399047735137095918" style="display:none">Marilynne Summers Robinson (born November 26, 1943) is an American novelist and essayist. Across her writing career, Robinson has received numerous awards, including the Pulitzer Prize for Fiction in 2005, National Humanities Medal in 2012, and the 2016 Library of Congress Prize for American Fiction. In 2016, Robinson was named in Time magazine's list of 100 most influential people.[2] Robinson began teaching at the Iowa Writers' Workshop in 1991[3] and retired in the spring of 2016.[4]<br /><br />Robinson is best known for her novels Housekeeping (1980) and Gilead (2004). Her novels are noted for their thematic depiction of both rural life and faith.[5] The subjects of her essays have spanned numerous topics, including the relationship between religion and science, US history, nuclear pollution, John Calvin, and contemporary American politics.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17399047735137095918" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
